“We submit the boat tomorrow,” I said. “We give them their perfect, compliant vessel. And then…”

“And then,” Lucia finished, folding the paper with the copied entries into a tiny, dense square, “we invite Giovanni Sarto to a conversation. We show him the architecture of his own power. And we ask him which stone he would like to remove before the whole wall falls on him.”

The hook for the next chapter was not a search, but a confrontation. It was the moment the needle, sharpened by probation and guided by a dead man’s sigh, would be pressed against the skin of the machine. We had built their boat. Now we would see if their secretary was made of sterner stuff than their timber. The battle was leaving the labyrinth of records. It was returning, armed with a specific, damning name, to the chamber where it had begun.



---

Chapter 16

CHAPTER 16

The morning of the submission dawned not with light, but with a slow, grey dilution of the dark. A scirocco had blown in during the night, a warm, damp breath from the south that carried the taste of salt and distant deserts. It slicked the stones of the courtyard, made the gondolas at their posts nudge and fret against their ropes like restless horses. The air in the workshop was thick, the scent of varnish and cured wood lying heavy, unable to escape through the shuttered windows.

The sandolo sat in the centre of the room, a creature of perfect, silent potential. The early light, what little there was, slid along its gunwales, caught the subtle ripple of its planking, the satin sheen of its finish. It was a beautiful forgery. We had built their lie so well it had become a kind of truth, a vessel that obeyed the false laws of their corrupt specification with a grace that was almost an insult. Matteo ran a cloth along its sheer, a final, unnecessary caress. His face was set, the planes of it sharp in the gloom.

“It is a good boat,” he said, his voice low. “It is a shame.”

Lucia stood by the door, a wrapped parcel under her arm. It contained not the dead notary’s journal, but her transcription, the architecture of the crime rendered in her precise, cipher-like hand. She wore a dress of deep grey, the colour of a pigeon’s wing, and her hair was bound tightly back. She looked less like a woman and more like a blade, honed and sheathed.

“The shame is theirs,” I said, checking the lines that would secure the boat to the transport barge. “We are merely returning it to them. With interest.”

The procession to the Arsenale was a sombre parody of a launch. There was no music, no garlands, no proud family. Just Matteo and I, poling the flat barge through the mist-choked canals, the sandolo cradled in its centre like a sleeping prince. Lucia walked the fondamenta, keeping pace, her figure a grey ghost between the damp-stained walls of the palaces. The city’s noise was muffled, absorbed by the wool of the fog. The slap of our pole, the drip of water from a rusted iron gutter, the low groan of the barge—these were the only sounds.

The great gate of the Arsenale loomed out of the mist, the winged lion of San Marco staring down with blank stone eyes. The guards, recognising the boat from the previous inspection, waved us through with bored indifference. The vast, enclosed basin of the dockyard was a cathedral of industry shrouded in vapour. The skeletons of galleys rose in the haze like the ribs of leviathans. The hammering of caulkers, the shriek of saws, the shouted orders—all were softened, made distant and dreamlike by the scirocco. We moved through a world of ghosts and half-made things.

We berthed at the assigned quay before the Office of the Provveditori. The building was a severe block of Istrian stone, its windows narrow, its doorway framed by columns that spoke of Roman severity rather than Venetian flourish. Two clerks in dark robes emerged, their footsteps echoing on the wet flagstones. They carried a measuring rod, a plumb line, a ledger.

The inspection was a silent, meticulous pantomime. They measured the length, the beam, the depth. They checked the rake of the stem and stern against their brass-bound specification scroll. They ran their hands along the seams, feeling for the tell-tale ridge of a poorly set plank. They tapped the hull with small wooden mallets, listening for the solid, uniform report of sound timber. Matteo and I stood aside, our hands clasped behind our backs. We did not speak. The boat spoke for us. It was, in every quantifiable dimension, perfect.

The head clerk, a man with a face like a closed ledger, made a final note. He looked from the boat to us, his expression unreadable. “It conforms.”

Two words. They hung in the damp air, the official seal on our deception. The clerks turned and walked back into the stone throat of the building, their duty discharged. The sandolo was no longer ours. It belonged to the Republic, to the machine, to the silent, hungry account books of Giovanni Sarto.

“Now,” Lucia said, materialising at my elbow. Her voice was dry, clear, cutting through the muffled yard. “We pay our respects to the secretary.”

The office of Tommaso Fedele was not in the grand, vaulted chambers of the Provveditori, but in a warren of administrative cells on the building’s second floor. The corridor was narrow, lit by high, grimy windows that did little to dispel the gloom. The air smelled of dust, old paper, and the faint, metallic tang of ink. The sound of a quill scratching, frantic and steady as a beetle in a box, came from behind a plain oak door.

Lucia knocked. The scratching stopped.

A voice, thin and strained, called out. “Enter.”

The room was a cage of paper. Parchment scrolls lay in cascading rolls on the floor. Stacks of ledgers and correspondence formed precarious towers on every surface. The desk was a landscape of documents, weighted down with ink pots and a smooth, grey stone. Behind it sat Tommaso Fedele. He was a small man, swallowed by his robe of office. His face was pale, the skin stretched tight over delicate bones, his eyes large and dark behind lenses of polished quartz that magnified their perpetual, watery anxiety. A single, expensive goose quill, its nib worn to a fine point, lay across the ledger he had been writing in.

“Master di Pietro,” he said, blinking. His gaze flicked to Matteo, then to Lucia, lingering on her with a mixture of confusion and instinctive unease. “The… the inspection is concluded? The boat is accepted?”

“It is, Secretary Fedele,” I said, closing the door softly behind us. The room seemed to shrink further. “A flawless conformity. You should be pleased. Your specifications were followed to the letter.”

A faint, nervous smile touched his lips. “The Republic’s specifications. I am merely their scribe.”

“A vital function,” Lucia said. Her voice was not unkind, but it had the cool, assessing tone of a physician. She moved into the room, not aggressively, but with a quiet ownership that made Fedele shrink back slightly in his chair. “The hand that records the law is the hand that gives it shape. Without you, the Provveditori would be shouting orders into the wind.”

He nodded, the motion jerky. “It is a duty. A solemn trust.” He gestured to the chaos around him. “The details… they are everything. A single digit misplaced, a measurement misrecorded… it can unravel months of work. Cost the Republic dearly.”

“Indeed,” I said. I leaned forward, placing my hands on the edge of his desk. The towers of paper trembled. “It is a curious thing, detail. It can build a galley that wins a war. Or it can build a sandolo that sinks a man.”

The room went very still. The only sound was the distant, muted thump of a mallet from the yards below. Fedele’s magnified eyes widened. The nervous smile froze, then melted away. “I… I do not take your meaning, Master.”

Lucia drew the folded square of paper from her sleeve. She did not hand it to him. She merely held it between her thumb and forefinger, a small, white cipher. “We have been in the archives, Secretary. The Archivio di Stato. A labyrinth. But labyrinths are made to be navigated, if one has a thread.”

Fedele’s breath hitched. His gaze was locked on the paper.

“We found a particular thread,” I continued, my voice dropping to the level of the dust motes swirling in the grey light. “A record of a timber shipment. Oak, from the Cadore forests. Grade One, kiln-dried. Ordered for the sandolo project. Signed by you.”

“That is standard,” he whispered. “Procedure.”

“It is,” Lucia agreed. She took a half-step closer. “But the thread led to another record. A payment, from the Republic’s purse, for that same Grade One oak. A substantial sum. And then, the thread led to a warehouse receipt, for the timber actually delivered to our squero. A different grade. A cheaper cut. Air-dried, not kilned. The difference in cost was… notable.”

Fedele’s hands had begun to shake. He clasped them together on the ledger, the knuckles white.

“The thread,” I said, “did not end there. It became a rope. It led to a journal. A private, lamentable record. Belonging to a notary named Alvise Contarini. A man burdened by his part in a scheme. A man who, before his unfortunate passing, named the hand that guided the pen. The hand that altered the shipment orders after the Provveditori’s seal was applied. The hand that approved the fraudulent invoices. The hand that ensured the specified timber was paid for, but never arrived.”

I let the silence swell, filled only by the secretary’s shallow, ragged breathing.

“He called you il ricopiatore,” Lucia said softly. “The copyist. The one who makes the clean, final draft from the messy original. In this case, the original was truth. The final draft was a fiction, written for profit. And the profit… did not flow to you alone, did it?”

Fedele seemed to crumple inward. The formal rigidity of his posture collapsed. He was a man made of parchment, and the words we spoke were a flame held too close. “You don’t understand,” he breathed, the words barely audible. “The pressure… the expectations. It is not a single request. It is a current. You either swim with it, or you are drowned by it.”

“Who?” The word was a needle, jabbed by Matteo from his place by the door. His first utterance in the room.

Fedele flinched. His magnified eyes swam with a film of tears—of fear, of exhaustion, of long-suppressed shame. “It is not a ‘who’. It is a… a function. A channel. The orders come. They are not shouted. They are implied. A suggestion in a meeting. A note appended to a document. A sigh of disappointment over a budget report. The specification for your sandolo… it was a test. A small, contained test. To see if the channel could be… irrigated. The timber was the first trickle. The boat itself, its flawed design meant to fail and discredit you, that was the second. You were not just a builder. You were a block in the canal. You needed to be removed, or made to pay toll.”

“Giovanni Sarto,” I stated.

Fedele let out a sound that was half-laugh, half-sob. “Sarto is a valve. A important one. He opens, he closes. He regulates the flow. But he is not the source. The source is the system itself. The need for the Arsenale to run, for the galleys to be built, for the accounts to show strength even as the timbers grow soft. My sin was not greed. It was efficiency. I saw a way to make the numbers balance, to give the Provveditori the report they desired, and to… to keep a little water for my own parched garden.” He looked at the paper in Lucia’s hand. “Is that Contarini’s confession?”

“It is a copy of its heart,” she said.

“He was a weak man,” Fedele murmured, a sudden, surprising venom in his voice. “He could not bear the weight of the compromise. I can. I have. For years.” The venom faded, replaced by a hollow defeat. “What will you do?”

“That depends,” I said, straightening up. “The boat is delivered. The fraud is documented. We have the story, from the first loosened stone to the last. We could take this to the Council of Ten. We could unravel the whole tapestry, starting with your thread.”

He closed his eyes. “I would be destroyed. My family would be ruined.”

“Yes,” Lucia said, without pity.

“There is another current,” I said. He opened his eyes. “You spoke of irrigation. Of channels. You are a man who understands the flow of paper, of orders, of secrets. You wish to keep your garden? Then you must become a different kind of conduit.”

I leaned in again, my shadow falling over his desk. “The sandolo is perfect. It will not fail. That part of the scheme is ended. You will report this to Sarto. You will tell him the di Pietro workshop has passed its test, that we are compliant, capable. That we are, in fact, an asset. You will ensure no more ‘tests’ are directed our way. You will, when you can, see that legitimate work flows to our squero.”

“And in return?” he whispered.

“In return,” Lucia said, folding the paper back into her sleeve, “this copy remains a copy. The original sigh of the dead notary stays in its crate. And your name remains where it is—on documents, not on a sentencing docket. But you are now our scribe, Secretary Fedele. You will record for us. You will note the orders that come, the suggestions made, the sighs of disappointment. You will tell us who speaks, and what they say. You will become our thread into the labyrinth.”

The demand hung in the paper-choked air. It was not a request for a single betrayal, but for a permanent, silent inversion of his loyalty. He would remain in his cage, but now he would sing for a different keeper.

He looked at his hands, at the ledger before him, at the world of ink and implication that was his entire life. He was a man built for detail, for surviving within lines. We had not asked him to step outside them, only to redraw them slightly, in our favour. The terror in his eyes slowly banked, replaced by a grim, calculating acceptance. It was a compromise he understood.

“The sandolo,” he said finally, his voice regaining a shred of its official thinness. “It is a fine vessel. Your work is commendable. I will see that the Provveditori’s report reflects this. And I… I am meticulous in my files. I keep notes on all matters of procedure. For clarity.”

“For clarity,” I echoed.

We left him then, surrounded by his monuments of paper. The frantic scratching of his quill did not resume as we closed the door. He was sitting in the silence, learning the new shape of his cage.

Outside, the scirocco had not lifted. The mist was now a steady, fine rain, warm and insistent. It beaded on the wool of our shoulders, dripped from the gargoyles. We walked back through the Arsenale, past the spectral half-ships.

“He will do it,” Matteo said, his voice gruff. “He is a creature of his desk. He will choose the path that lets him keep scratching in his ledger.”

“He is a crack in their wall,” Lucia said. “A small one. But we have placed our wedge in it. Now we wait for the pressure to tell.”

We reached our empty barge. The space where the sandolo had lain was a void of wet planking. We had given them their perfect, compliant vessel. We had turned their corrupt scribe. The battle had shifted, from defence to a tense, silent occupation of enemy ground.

But as I poled the barge back into the misty canal, the weight of Fedele’s words settled on me. Sarto is a valve. He is not the source. We had pressed our needle against the machine and found a man who was more conduit than commander. We had stopped one small, directed attack. But the current he spoke of—the deep, institutional hunger that turned good timber to bad, that turned honest men into copyists of lies—that current still flowed. It had merely diverted around our new obstruction.

The hook for the next chapter was not relief, but a deeper, colder understanding. We had won a skirmish in a paper war. But the real enemy was not a man, or even a conspiracy of men. It was the rot in the timber, the sigh in the archive, the silent, accepted compromise that made the whole edifice stand. We had plugged one leak. The canal was still full of water, and it was still rising.



---

Chapter 17

CHAPTER 17

The rain was a veil, turning the city into a series of grey impressions. The domes of the Salute were smudges of lead, the lantern of the campanile a drowned star. I poled the barge through the murk, the silence between us as thick as the mist that coiled on the water’s surface. We had left a man broken on the wheel of his own conscience. The victory was ash in the mouth.

Lucia stood at the prow, a statue in a sodden cloak. Matteo worked the second pole, his movements a slow, punishing rhythm. The only sounds were the drip of water from the poles, the suck of the canal against the hull, the distant, mournful call of a boatman from some other, unseen thoroughfare.

“A valve,” I said, the word a ghost in the damp air. It was not a question.

“A valve controls pressure,” Lucia replied, without turning. “It does not generate it. Fedele let something through. He did not create the flow.”

Matteo grunted, driving his pole deep into the silty bed. “So we find the pump. The heart of the thing.”

But that was the chill Fedele had left in my bones. What if there was no single heart? What if the machine was the city itself, its every joint and tendon flexing with the same slow, corrupt purpose? The Arsenalotti’s loyalty twisted into blind pride, the merchants’ greed dressed as progress, the clerks’ cowardice masquerading as order. A conspiracy required conspirators. What we faced felt more like a climate, a season of decay that made every choice seem inevitable, every compromise sensible.

We moored the barge at our usual post, the ropes slick in my hands. The workshop was a cave of shadows, the fire in the hearth long dead. The smell of cold resin and damp stone was a greeting. I struck a flint to the tinder, coaxing a small, resentful flame to life. Its light did not cheer; it merely carved out the dimensions of our gloom.

Matteo busied himself with the stove, his back a wall. Lucia shed her cloak, hanging it by the feeble heat. She drew out the small, wax-stoppered bottle from her sleeve—the one she had taken from the Greek’s sandolo. She placed it on the workbench beside the unfinished viol. The two objects sat in silent conference: one a vessel for beauty, the other a vessel for an ending.

“We must understand the dose,” she said, her voice clinical. “We know its purpose. We must now know its method.”

I fetched a clean scrap of parchment, a quill, a dish of water. She unstoppered the bottle. The smell was faint, a ghost of bitter almonds and something else, metallic, like the air after a lightning strike. With the meticulous care of an apothecary, she let a single drop fall onto the parchment. It beaded, oily and dark.

“It is not a brawler’s poison,” she murmured, leaning close. “It is not for a tavern brawl or a jealous husband. It is a diplomat’s tool. A quiet closing of a door.”

Matteo brought over a mug of warmed wine. He looked at the droplet. “How does it work?”

“Through the skin, I would wager,” I said, remembering the Greek’s gloved hands. “Or perhaps inhaled. A fine mist in a confined space. A handshake. A sealed room.”

“Efficient,” Matteo said, the word grim with approval.

“It is innovation,” Lucia corrected, her eyes fixed on the dark bead. “A perversion of craft. The alchemist’s art turned to this. Not to transmute lead to gold, but to turn breath into silence.”

Tradition. Innovation. The lens through which we had viewed our own work—the struggle between my father’s methods and the demands of a changing world—now showed its darker counterpart. The Arsenal’s tradition was rot and cover-up. Its innovation was this: a poison refined, a corruption made elegant and precise.

I thought of the new hulls in the squero, built with bad timber. A traditional failure, born of greed and neglect. And I thought of this bottle, a product of a different, more vicious ingenuity. Both would sink a ship. One slowly, with a groan of protesting wood. The other instantly, with a final, unuttered word.

“We need an alchemist,” I said.

Lucia nodded. “Not a university man. Not an official. Someone who works in the margins. Someone who knows the recipes that are not written in books.”

Matteo’s face darkened. “The streghe of the Ghetto Nuovo? The charlatans by the slaughterhouses?”

“The people who know things the Council of Ten would rather remained unknown,” Lucia said. She carefully folded the parchment around the droplet, sealing the poison in a paper tomb. “The knowledge we seek does not sit on a public shelf.”

It was a dangerous turning. To move from the world of clerks and contracts, of timber and tariffs, into the shadow-world of forbidden arts. We were violin-makers, a scribe, a boatman. We were not equipped for this darker commerce.

But the hook Fedele had set in me would not dislodge. Sarto is a valve. We had to trace the pipe back to its source, even if that source was a cesspit.

“I will go,” I said. “The Ghetto is not so far. I have traded for varnish materials there before. Rosin. Certain oils. It has a gate, but it is not a prison. They will ask fewer questions of a craftsman with silver.”

“You will not go alone,” Matteo stated.

“Two of us will draw more attention in those narrow streets. One man on an errand is invisible. Two are a delegation.” I met his stubborn gaze. “I will be a customer. Nothing more.”

Lucia studied me, her gaze weighing the risk. “Take the pupparin,” she said finally. “It is small, fast. Be back before the midnight bell. If you are not, we come for you.”

The pupparin was a sleek, black blade of a boat, stored under a tarpaulin at the rear of the workshop. I readied it while Matteo, grumbling, prepared a small purse of coin and a handful of my less-identifiable tools—a potential offering for trade. The rain had softened to a drizzle, the mist now lying in ragged sheets over the water.

I pushed off, the narrow boat responding like a living thing. I slipped into the network of smaller canals, the high, windowless walls of the Ghetto rising before me. The gate was shut but not guarded; a knock, a few coins passed to the night watchman through a slot, and the small door within the great one swung open. The silence inside was of a different quality than the city’s—not empty, but watchful, compressed by the high buildings. The streets were canyons, the washing strung between balconies like spectral banners.

I knew the shop: a low door under a sign showing a mortar and pestle. The man who kept it was named Elia. He was old, his beard a cloud of white, his eyes the colour of old sherry. He sold remedies, perfumes, the raw ingredients for inks and paints. He asked no questions of those who could pay.

The bell above his door chimed a thin, tired note. The shop smelled of dust, dried herbs, and camphor. Elia emerged from a back room, wiping his hands on a cloth. His eyes, sharp in their wrinkled nests, took me in.

“Master di Pietro,” he said, his voice a dry rustle. “You are far from your squero. Your varnish stocks are low?”

“I seek knowledge, Master Elia. Not materials.”

His gaze grew still. “Knowledge is a more expensive commodity. And a more dangerous one to transport.”

I placed the folded parchment on the counter between us. “I need to understand what this is. Its composition. Its origin, if possible.”

He did not touch it. “You bring a stranger’s child to my door and ask for its lineage. This is not a service I provide.”

I set the purse of silver beside the parchment. Then I added the small, fine-toothed saw, its blade gleaming in the lamplight. A beautiful tool, useful for inlay, for delicate work.

He glanced at the saw. A craftsman’s appreciation flickered in his eyes, then was gone. “You are a good maker. You should not be digging in graves. The soil there is poisoned.”

“The poison is already above ground,” I said quietly. “It is being used. I must know its nature to know its master.”

He was silent for a long moment, his eyes on the folded paper. With a sigh that seemed to come from the depths of his bones, he reached below the counter and brought out a sheet of glass, a slender brass probe, and a magnifying lens. With infinite care, he opened the parchment. The dark droplet glistened.

He brought the lens close. He did not smell it. He merely looked, his breathing shallow. After a minute, he straightened.

“Where did you get this?”

“Does it matter?”

“It tells me how brave, or how foolish, you are.” He tapped the glass near, but not touching, the poison. “This is not Venetian. Not Italian. This is a refinement of a Byzantine formula. The Greeks called it ‘aer silens’—the silent air. It is distilled from a particular lichen that grows only on tombs in the Peloponnese, combined with the essence of peach stones, and a… catalyst. A metal, dissolved in aqua fortis. It is not a poison of the gut. It is a poison of the blood. It stops the heart as if it had never beaten.”

The description was cold, precise. It matched the Greek’s fastidiousness, his air of ancient, patient malice. “Could it be made here?”

Elia’s lips thinned. “With the right knowledge, the right materials… perhaps. But why? We have simpler poisons. Hemlock. Arsenic. This… this is for a specific purpose. It leaves almost no trace. A physician might blame a sudden palsy, a failure of the humours. It is an assassin’s poem. Elegant. Untraceable.”

“And the catalyst? The metal?”

He looked at me, and for the first time, I saw a flicker of fear in his old eyes. “That is the signature. It is not lead, not mercury. It is a new thing. A by-product of the furnaces where they try to make better cannon. A waste metal, they thought, until someone discovered its… affinity for human blood. The Arsenale produces it. In small, secret quantities.”

The connection clicked into place with the cold finality of a lock. The Arsenale. The source of the pressure. It was not just corrupt timber and stolen wages. It was a laboratory for this new, silent evil. The same institution that built the Republic’s might was now refining the tools for its secret murder.

“Who would know this?” I pressed. “Who there would have this knowledge?”

Elia carefully refolded the parchment, pushing it back toward me as if it were a live coal. “I do not traffic in names. I have told you what the child is. I will not point you to its father. Leave now. And do not come back with such questions.”

The dismissal was absolute. I took the parchment and my tools, leaving the silver on the counter. He did not thank me. He simply turned and vanished into the dark of his back room, the bell chiming my exit into the damp, watchful night.

The return journey was a blur. The weight of the knowledge was heavier than any timber. The Arsenale was not just compromised; it was actively malignant. A valve, Fedele had said. But the pump was not a person. It was a system that now produced, alongside its warships, a refined product of death.

I slipped the pupparin back into its berth. The workshop light still burned. Lucia and Matteo were waiting, their faces tense in the firelight.

“Well?” Matteo demanded.

I told them. The Greek poison. Its Byzantine heritage. The Arsenale’s unique, metallic signature. The silence that followed was profound.

“So the heart of the machine,” Lucia said slowly, “is not an office. It is a foundry. A furnace.”

“We cannot storm a furnace,” Matteo growled. “We cannot reason with fire and molten metal.”

“No,” I agreed, the cold understanding settling into a hard, clear plan. “But we can follow the product. The poison did not leave the Arsenale in a barrel. It left in that bottle. It was given to the Greek. By someone. A transfer. A transaction. Fedele processed the paperwork for the sandolo. Who processed the paperwork for this?”

We had been looking for men of influence, men of command. But in a machine this vast, the most dangerous parts were often the small, greased gears—the quartermasters, the storekeepers, the clerks of the special inventories. The men who knew where the unmarked barrels were stored, who signed for materials that had no official name.

We had a wedge in Fedele. He was a clerk of the docks. But the poison spoke of a different department altogether: the Provveditori all’Arsenale, the overseers of the factories and stores. Their records would not be in Fedele’s damp archive. They would be in the heart of the fortress itself, in the Casa dell’Armamento, the House of Armament.

To get there, we would need more than a frightened scribe. We would need a ghost. We would need to walk into the very belly of the machine, while it was awake and working.

Lucia saw the resolve forming on my face. “You are thinking of the night inspection,” she said. “The fire watch.”

Every third night, a team of Arsenalotti made a round of the foundries and storehouses, checking for embers, for leaks, for sabotage. They wore specific insignia. They moved on a schedule.

“We need the uniforms,” I said. “The tokens. The route.”

Matteo’s grin was a fierce, sharp thing in the gloom. “Now you are talking a language I understand. Theft.”

“Observation first,” Lucia cautioned. “Then theft. Then entry. One misstep, and we are not merely intruders. We are spies in a military arsenal. The punishment is not prison. It is a noose from the campanile.”

The hook for the next chapter was not a question, but a threshold. We had traced the venom to its nest. Now we had to don the skin of the beast and walk inside. The battle was no longer on paper, or even on the water. It was in the fire-lit dark, under the shadow of the great roofs where the Republic forged its power and its poisons. We would go into the dragon’s throat, and pray its senses were dulled by its own smoke.



---

Chapter 18

CHAPTER 18

The uniforms were not stolen. They were borrowed from the dead.

The realisation came not in a flash, but as a slow, cold seep, like water finding the flaw in a hull. We had spent three days watching the fire-watch teams from the high, shadowed galleries of the ropewalk, where the air was thick with the smell of hemp and tar. We noted their rounds, their lanterns, the specific cut of their grey cioppe, the workman’s coats. We saw the lead man carry a brass token, flashing it at each checkpoint. Theft, as Matteo had suggested, was impossible; the teams changed each watch, collecting their gear from a guarded room in the Casa del Cane, the House of the Dog, before their rounds, and returning it after.

“They are not just uniforms,” Lucia whispered on the third evening, her eyes fixed on the departing team. “They are shrouds. Each set is accounted for, like a weapon. To take one from the living is to sound an alarm before we even begin.”

It was Matteo, prowling the forgotten edges of the Arsenale Vecchio, who found the solution. In a disused storehouse, its roof sagging under a mat of moss, he discovered a cache of old equipment. Barrels of dried-up pitch, rusted tools, and in a mildewed chest, three folded cioppe. They were of an older pattern, the grey faded to the colour of ash, but serviceable. They smelled of camphor and neglect. More importantly, they bore the faint, embroidered insignia of the Provveditori’s office—a winged lion holding a sword and a set of scales—still visible in the gloom.

“Not stolen,” Matteo said, holding one up. It released a cloud of dust. “Resurrected. Who will question a ghost?”

The tokens were a harder problem. They were brass, freshly stamped each month. We could not forge them. But we could, perhaps, borrow one for just long enough to have a duplicate cast. That task fell to Lucia. She tracked the chief of the night watch, a grizzled Arsenalotto named Bruno, to a tavern in Castello. She watched him drink, watched him play morra, slamming his fist on the table as he shouted numbers. She saw him, at the evening’s end, fumble with his purse and drop the brass disc, retrieving it with a grunt before stumbling out into the night.

“He is a creature of habit,” she reported. “And his habits are drink and debt. He visits a moneylender near the Greek church every Friday after his watch. He will be distracted, and angry.”

The plan was a house of cards, each piece relying on the one below. On Friday, I would be at the moneylender’s, a dispute over a fictional debt ready on my lips, to collide with Bruno and provide a greater distraction. Lucia would do the lifting. Matteo would be waiting with a master forger in a cellar near the Ghetto, a man who worked in soft lead and could produce a passable copy in two hours. We would return the original to Bruno’s path before he noticed its absence.

It worked. The collision was messy, my protestations loud. Bruno’s face, already florid, darkened with rage. As he shoved me against a wall, spittle flying with his curses, Lucia’s hand—light as a pickpocket’s, which she had once been—dipped into the pouch at his belt and emerged with the cool, sharp-edged disc. Two hours later, as Bruno sat scowling in a different tavern, nursing a bruised knuckle and his indignation, the copy was pressed into my palm. It was warm from the casting. The detail was soft, the lion’s wings blurred, but it would pass in the dim, flickering light of a storehouse lantern.

We had our skins. We had our key.

The false victory was not in obtaining them. It was in the belief that this was the hard part. That the machine, for all its size, was blind to three ghosts in old coats. We had traced the poison to a department. We had a plan to walk its halls. We felt, in the cramped safety of my workshop, a grim and gleaming certainty. We were innovators, outthinking the stagnant traditions of a sclerotic state. We would use its own rituals, its own uniforms, against it.

We were fools.

The night we chose was moonless, the sky a bowl of wet black wool. A scirocco had blown in, bringing not rain but a clinging damp that muffled sound and painted every stone and timber with a cold sweat. We dressed in the workshop. The old cioppe were stiff, the smell of camphor replaced now by our own scents of nervous salt and dried rosemary—Lucia’s doing, a superstition against evil. We blackened our faces with charcoal, our hands with soot.

“Remember,” Lucia said, her voice low and flat. “We are the third team. We follow the route of the second after a half-hour delay. We do not speak unless challenged. If we are challenged, we cough and point to our throats—the scirocco has stolen our voices. We look at the ground. We are tired men, thinking of our beds.”

Matteo adjusted the worn cap on his head, his usual grin absent. He looked like what he was supposed to be: a sullen, bone-tired labourer. I felt the weight of the false token in my inner pocket, a lie pressed against my ribs.

We entered through the Porta Magna, the water-gate, but not as ourselves. We slipped in with a returning barge from the Lido, laden with firewood for the foundries, crouching among the logs until the barge nudged the interior quay. We disembarked with the bargemen, heads down, and melted into the perpetual twilight of the Arsenal’s interior.

The scale of the place at night was not diminished; it was transformed. The daylight chaos of shouting men and crashing timber was gone, replaced by a deeper, more profound roar. The foundries never slept. Their glow painted the undersides of the vast roofs a hellish orange, and the air throbbed with the beat of trip-hammers—a deep, rhythmic thud-thud-thud that vibrated in the teeth and the marrow. Sparks fountained against the blackness like inverted constellations. It was the heartbeat of the beast, and we were walking into its ventricles.

We saw the first fire-watch team ahead, their lanterns bobbing like will-o’-the-wisps as they trudged past the great stacks of seasoned oak. We ducked into the lee of a half-caulked hull, waiting for their light to fade. Then we began.

The Casa dell’Armamento was not a single building, but a complex of storehouses and offices built against the sheer inner wall of the Arsenale Nuovo. Its doors were heavy oak, banded with iron. The first guard was a young man, huddled in a niche for the wind, his pike leaning beside him. I flashed the token. He glanced at the blurred lion, then at our faces, his own a mask of bored misery. He nodded, waving us past with a grunt that was swallowed by the hammer-song.

The second door was inside, at the head of a corridor lined with ledgers on shelves that reached the ceiling. Here, the guard was older, sharper. His eyes, small and bright like a rat’s, scanned us. He held out a hand for the token, turning it over in his fingers, bringing it close to his candle.

“This month’s stamp is faint,” he said, his voice a rasp.

I coughed, a raw, grating sound, and pointed to my throat. Lucia beside me did the same, a pathetic duet. Matteo merely stared at the floor, a picture of dumb exhaustion.

The guard’s eyes narrowed. He looked from the token to my soot-blackened face. The moment stretched, filled with the distant, rhythmic thudding. Then, with a shrug that seemed to dismiss us as beneath further thought, he handed the token back. “The damp rots the ink,” he muttered, more to himself than to us, and pulled the door open.

We were in.

The records room of the Provveditori was a cathedral of paper. The shelves here were canyon walls, illuminated by guttering sconces. The air was dry and carried the odour of old ink, parchment, and the faint, sweet smell of the glue used in bindings. It was a world away from the fiery, pounding vitality outside. This was the beast’s memory, cold, precise, and vast.

We had agreed to search for the special inventories—the partite speciali. Lucia went to the most recent ledgers, her fingers flying over the spines. Matteo and I moved deeper into the forest of shelves, looking for older records, for the origins of the trail.

I found them in a dim corner, a series of folios bound in black leather, unmarked. I pulled one down. The pages were filled with columns: dates, codes, quantities, and a single word for the receiving official. The entries were mundane at first: brass wire, fine-grade, 100 lbs. White lead, 20 barrels. Linseed oil, 50 flasks. Then, nestled between orders for lamp-black and varnish, I saw it.

Cinnabar, refined, 5 lbs. For: Verification of gilding adherence. Received by: D. B.

My heart clenched. Cinnabar. The source of mercury. The entry was from over a year ago. The code beside it matched the one Fedele had shown us from the dockyard ledger. This was the link. The poison had been ordered here, under the guise of a material for verifying the gold leaf on stern-galleries or figureheads. A lie so technical, so boring, it would never be questioned.

“Lorenzo,” Lucia’s whisper was a blade in the silence. She was at my shoulder, holding a newer ledger open. Her finger rested on an entry from six months prior. Orpiment, 3 lbs. For: Testing of new yellow pigment for pennants. Received by: D. B.

Arsenic.

And below it, another. Nux vomica, extract, 1 flask. For: Rat eradication in grain stores. Received by: D. B.

Strychnine.

All under the same pretence—mundane, technical, trivial. All received by the same initials: D.B.

“He is not a clerk,” I breathed, the truth dawning with a sickening clarity. “A clerk would not have access across years, across different departments. He is one of the Provveditori themselves. Or someone so high in their office he might as well be.”

This was our false victory. We had thought we were hunting a functionary, a corrupt gear. We had found the hand that greased the gears. The innovation was not in some new, hidden plot. It was in the brazen, traditional use of the system itself. The tradition was the cover. The ledgers were not a secret; they were official record. The orders were not hidden; they were filed. The poison was not smuggled; it was inventoried. The deepest treachery was not an innovation, but a perversion of tradition so complete it became invisible.

We had to find a name. We searched for a log of officials, a roster. Matteo, scaling a ladder to a high shelf, found a vellum-bound book titled “Doveri e Designazioni, Ufficio dei Provveditori all’Arsenale, 1570-1575.”

He brought it down. We huddled around a single candle. The pages listed appointments, promotions, assignments. My finger traced down the list of names and initials. And there it was.

Daniele Barbaro, appointed Sotto-Provveditore, Fabriche Chimiche e Materiali Speciali, April 1572.

D. B.

A Sotto-Provveditore. An under-overseer. A man with the authority to request, to sign for, to dispose of anything within his purview. A man who could, with a stroke of his pen, turn state-owned poison into a weapon for a private, murderous war.

“Barbaro,” Lucia said. The name hung in the dry air.

It was a good Venetian name. An old name. A name that could belong to a man in a silk robe, sitting on the Grand Council.

The shock of it, the sheer, bureaucratic audacity, held us for a moment in silence. It was the silence that betrayed us.

We did not hear the door open. The first we knew was the wash of lantern light, flooding our aisle from both ends.

We turned. Four men stood at one end of the canyon of shelves. Four more at the other. They were not night watchmen. They wore the dark, sober cloaks of the Capi di Contrada, the internal security of the Arsenal. Their faces were hard, professional, devoid of boredom. They carried short, heavy clubs.

And they were not alone.

Between the two groups, stepping forward into the circle of our meagre candlelight, was a man in a long grey cioppa of fine, new wool. His face was narrow, intelligent, cleanshaven. His hair was silver at the temples. He held no weapon. In his hands was a ledger, its cover identical to the one I held.

Daniele Barbaro.

He did not look surprised. He looked like a master craftsman inspecting a flawed piece of work.

“You have the 1573 folio,” he said, his voice calm, almost professorial. “I would recommend the 1574. The entries are more… illustrative.”

My hand went to my inner pocket, not for the token, but for the knife at my belt. Barbaro saw the movement and gave a slight, dismissive shake of his head.

“Please. There are eight of them. You are in the heart of a fortress. The sound of a struggle here would bring fifty more.” He closed his ledger with a soft thump. “We have been watching Fedele the dock clerk for weeks. A man in debt develops new habits, new associates. We wondered who would come to follow the trail. Frankly, I expected agents of the Ten. Not a luthier, a street-thief, and a disgraced soldier.” He glanced at each of us in turn, his gaze lingering on Lucia. “Innovative, I grant you. Using the old uniforms was a nice touch. A traditional form put to a novel use. But you see,” he continued, taking a step closer, “the tradition is stronger. It has more eyes. It has patience.”

This was the shattering. Our house of cards, our clever plan, our borrowed skins—it was all known. We had not outwitted the machine. We had walked into a waiting hand.

“You poisoned the Cavalli,” I said, the words ash in my mouth.

“I procured materials for the security of the Republic,” Barbaro corrected softly. “Their application… was outsourced. A necessary pruning. The Cavalli were innovators of the most dangerous kind. They believed their new ships, their new finances, could bypass the old structures. They threatened a balance that has kept Venice alive for centuries. My family has served that balance for just as long.”

He was not a fanatic. He was a custodian. A preserver. In his eyes, he was the tradition, cutting out the infection of reckless innovation. We were not heroes uncovering a plot. We were a symptom of the disease he was eradicating.

“You will hang for it,” Matteo growled.

Barbaro smiled, a thin, cold curve of the lips. “I think not. You are intruders in the secret heart of the Arsenal’s armament. You are spies, caught with stolen insignia, tampering with the most sensitive records of the state. You were discovered by the vigilant Capi di Contrada. There will be a brief hearing at dawn. The evidence is,” he gestured around us, “overwhelming. And then you will hang from the campanile in the Piazza, as a lesson to all who would think to pierce the Republic’s secrets.”

He nodded to the guards. “Take them to the holding cells in the Casa del Cane. Search them thoroughly. I will prepare the deposition for the Council of Ten.”

The guards moved in. Resistance was futile, a quick and brutal beating before the same end. I felt the hard grip on my arms, the search that found my knife, the false token. I saw the same happen to Matteo, to Lucia. Her eyes met mine, not with fear, but with a furious, calculating intensity. She was not defeated. She was counting, measuring, waiting.

As they marched us out of the records room, back into the hammering dark, the false victory was complete. We had found the source, and the source had found us. We had believed the battle was in the doing, in the clever entry. We were wrong. The battle had always been in the knowing. And now, Daniele Barbaro knew us. The tradition had not been sleeping. It had been watching, from within its own fortress, and it had let us walk right into its deepest, most silent cell.

The hook for the next chapter was not a threshold, but a cage. We were in the dragon’s throat, and it had not been dulled by smoke. It had swallowed us whole. Our only remaining innovation would be the manner of our digestion.



---

Chapter 19

CHAPTER 19

The Casa del Cane was not a place of howling, but of silence. It was a digestive silence, the quiet of stone absorbing sound, of thick, iron-studded doors swallowing protest, of straw on damp earth muffling the tread of the jailer’s boots. Our cell was a cavity in the fundament of the Arsenal’s outer wall, where the salt from the lagoon wept in rusty streaks down the bricks and the air held the cold, spent breath of the sea.

They had taken everything: my knife, the false insignia, the lock-picks from Lucia’s sleeves, even the thin-bladed shaving tool from Matteo’s boot. They left us with the clothes we stood in, which were now the damp uniforms of our own capture. The search had been efficient, impersonal. They had found what Barbaro expected them to find. They had not found what was not there to be found: a plan for this.

Lucia stood at the single, high slit of a window, a bar of grey, pre-dawn light cutting her face into planes of pallor and shadow. She was not looking out—the slit showed only a wedge of blank sky—but listening. Her fingers traced the mortar between the stones, not idly, but with the pressure of a lutenist checking the tension of a string.

“They will come at first light,” Matteo said. His voice was a low rumble in the cell, the sound of a saw held still against wood. He sat on the rough bench, his big hands dangling between his knees, curled into fists that had nothing to strike. “A hearing before the Capi di Contrada. A formality. Then the Piazza.”

I leaned against the wall opposite Lucia, the chill of it a steady ache through my shoulder blades. Barbaro’s words hung in the silence he had crafted for us. A lesson to all who would think to pierce the Republic’s secrets. We were not criminals to him. We were a correction. A necessary deletion from the text of tradition. Our innovation was our heresy, and heresy demanded a public burning, a hanging from the campanile to swing like a discordant bell.

“The hearing is not a formality,” I said. The words felt thick. “It is the first page of his deposition. He will read our crime into the record. Our intrusion, our tools, our disguises. He will make the lie official. Once it is writ, it becomes the truth. The state will have its narrative, neat and complete.”

“And we become a footnote,” Lucia said, without turning. “A cautionary tale. Three fools who thought they could outwit the Arsenal’s keepers.” Her tracing finger stopped. “He is not just a fanatic. He is an archivist. He understands the power of the record. He is writing our end into the ledger of Venetian justice. A closed account.”

The silence returned, deeper now. We were not just in a cell; we were inside the logic of Daniele Barbaro. The cage was not merely iron and stone, but story. He had authored our part: the blundering spies. He would author our conclusion: the public execution. Our voices, our truths, would be irrelevant. They would be the scratch of a broken quill, dismissed from the official page.

Matteo lifted his head. “Then we do not attend his hearing.”

“They will drag us,” I said.

“Not if we are not here.”

Lucia turned now. The grey light caught the sharpness of her cheekbone, the dark intensity of her eyes. “The door is three inches of oak. The lock is a serratura a doppia mordacchia, cast iron. The jailer has the only key, and he wears it on a chain around his neck. He checks the slit every half-hour. He is due.” Her voice was flat, factual. She had already inventoried our prison. It was what she did. She measured the world as a series of mechanisms, of problems to be solved.

“We have no tools,” I said.

“We have our hands,” Matteo said.

“Your hands cannot pick that lock. Nor can they break that door.”

“No,” Lucia said. A faint, almost imperceptible line appeared between her brows, the sign of her mind working at its fiercest pitch. “But they are not the only mechanism in this room.”

She pushed away from the wall and walked to the center of the cell, her gaze sweeping the stones of the floor, then climbing the walls to the vaulted ceiling where darkness pooled. “Barbaro is an archivist. He thinks in records, in preserved things. He sees us as an anomaly to be removed. But he is also a man of the Arsenal. He thinks in terms of systems. Of supply. Of function.” She knelt, brushing aside the mildewed straw. “This is not just a prison. It is part of a machine. The Casa del Cane houses those who threaten the machine. Therefore, it must be maintained. It must be fed. It must be… emptied.”

Her fingers found a gap between two flagstones, not a crack, but a deliberate channel, half-filled with silt and old straw. She dug at it. “The Arsenal does not waste. Even its waste has a purpose. Offal from the butcheries feeds the cats that hunt the rats. Sawdust from the squeri packs the joints of new hulls. What does it do with the waste from its prisoners?”

Matteo stood, understanding dawning. “The slops bucket.”

It sat in the corner, a foul, wooden thing. The jailer would empty it when he brought the dawn ration—water and hard biscuit. That was the routine. That was the system.

Lucia rose and went to it, her nose wrinkling only slightly. She did not look inside. Instead, she ran her hands over its exterior, then lifted it. The base was wet, ringed with a dark stain. She set it down again, carefully. “It is oak, like the door. Heavy. But it is not just a container. It is a part of the cycle. It leaves this cell. It returns, empty.” She looked at me, then at Matteo. “We cannot break the system. But we can… redirect its output.”

The idea was a spark in the digestive dark. Not a plan, not yet. A filament of possibility, thinner than a violin string and just as taut. We had no tools, but we had the components of our own captivity. We had the bucket. We had the straw. We had the coming of the jailer, as predictable as the tide. And we had the silence, which was not our enemy, but the medium in which we now had to work.

“When he comes,” I said, the words forming slowly, “he will be alone. He expects three defeated prisoners, awaiting their fate. He will unlock the door, enter to retrieve the bucket, perhaps leave the ration. His focus will be on the task, not on us. The system is his guard.”

Matteo’s fists uncurled. “He is a cog. He turns one way.”

“And we must make him slip,” Lucia finished. She began to gather the driest strands of straw from the edges of the cell, weaving them quickly, deftly into a short, tight cord. “Not a weapon. A distraction. A moment of imbalance.” She handed the crude braid to Matteo. “When he bends for the bucket, you will be here, by the bench, seeming broken. You will trip. Fall toward him. Not to attack. To entangle. To confuse.”

“And I?” I asked.

“You will be there,” she pointed to the shadowed space behind the door as it would swing open. “You are the thinnest of us. You will take the key.”

“He wears the chain.”

“Chains can break,” she said. “Under sudden, unexpected strain. A man falling, a body lurching—the clasp is not made for that kind of force. It is made for the gentle sway of a man walking his rounds. It is not made for chaos.”

It was a desperate calculus. It relied on the jailer’s complacency, on the precision of Matteo’s fall, on the weakness of a cheap clasp, on my own speed and silence. It was an innovation of desperation, built from the scraps of our cage. It was the opposite of Barbaro’s careful, recorded tradition. It was improvisation, and it had to be perfect.

We took our positions as the light through the slit warmed from grey to a sickly yellow. The sounds of the Arsenal waking filtered down to us—the distant clang of a hammer, a shouted order, the rumble of a cart. The machine was beginning its day. Our execution was being scheduled, the gallows-cart perhaps already being wheeled from a shed.

The heavy tread came right on the rhythm. Boots on stone, the jingle of a key. The sound stopped outside our door. The slit darkened as a face blocked it—a bored, broad face with a thick mustache, eyes scanning the cell. He saw Matteo on the bench, head down. He saw me, leaning against the far wall, eyes closed as if in despair. He did not see Lucia; she was flattened in the blind spot beside the door’s hinge.

The key rattled in the lock. The clunk of the heavy tumblers turning was the loudest sound in the world. The door groaned inward.

The jailer stepped inside, a lump of bread in one hand, a small jug in the other. His eyes went immediately to the slops bucket. The smell was part of his routine. He set the bread and jug on the bench near Matteo.

“On your feet,” he grunted, not to us, but to himself, a man talking to his work.

He bent, his fingers closing around the handle of the bucket.

Matteo moved.

It was not a lunge. It was a colossal, clumsy toppling, as if his legs had given way under the weight of his despair. He gave a grunt of surprise, his arms flailing. He fell into the jailer’s space, his shoulder driving into the man’s bent back. The straw cord in his hand snagged around the jailer’s boot, his wrist.

“Porco cane!” the jailer snarled, stumbling forward, the bucket tipping, its foul contents sloshing over his legs. He tried to straighten, to shove Matteo off, his hand going to the short baton at his belt.

In that second of shocked, disgusted imbalance, I moved from the shadows. Not at the man, but at the chain around his neck. My hands closed over the key where it lay against his soiled tunic. I pulled, not out, but down, with all my weight, adding to the force of Matteo’s fall.

There was a sharp, metallic ping.

The clasp gave.

The key and a fragment of chain were in my hand. The jailer, now roaring in full anger, threw Matteo aside and turned, his baton raised, his face a mask of fury. He saw me, saw the key in my grasp. His eyes widened with a pure, professional outrage. We had not just attacked him; we had broken the ritual.

He opened his mouth to bellow for the guards who would be stationed down the corridor.

Lucia’s hand clamped over it from behind. Her other arm snaked around his throat, not to choke, but to anchor. She was a shadow on his back, a spider on a thrashing fly. “The lock,” she hissed at me, her voice strained.

I was already at the door, pulling it shut. The world narrowed to the iron key, to the shape of the lock. My hands, used to fitting delicate purfling into curved wood, were shaking. I fumbled. The key scraped against the plate.

The jailer bucked, throwing Lucia against the wall. He spun, baton swinging. Matteo caught the blow on his forearm with a sickening crack, but he wrapped his other arm around the jailer’s waist, pinning the baton arm.

I tried again. The key found the hole. Turned. One tumbler. Another. The final clunk.

The door was locked. With us inside. With him.

The jailer froze, hearing that sound. The sound of his own system sealing him in with the anomalies. His rage turned to something colder, more desperate. He stopped struggling. He looked at the three of us, panting in the dim cell. He saw the key in the lock on the wrong side. He saw the broken chain on his chest. He understood. He was now the problem within the machine.

“You are dead men,” he whispered, the words dripping with venom. “You have only made it worse.”

“Perhaps,” I said, my breath raw in my throat. “But now you are in the story with us.”

We bound him with his own belt and strips torn from his tunic, gagged him with a wad of cleanish straw. He sat in the corner where the bucket had been, his eyes promising murder. Matteo cradled his arm, his face pale but set. Lucia retrieved the key from the lock.

“His relief will not come until midday,” she said, listening again at the door. “We have perhaps three hours. But we are still inside the Casa del Cane. We are still deep in the Arsenal. And every man out there is a potential guard for Barbaro’s tradition.”

We had broken one link in the chain. But the greater mechanism surrounded us, humming with purpose. We had escaped our cell, only to find ourselves in a larger, more intricate prison. We had the key to one door. We needed a map for a labyrinth.

Matteo nodded toward the jailer. “He has clothes. And an unbroken face.”

It was the next logical, desperate step. The jailer’s uniform was a kind of key itself—a pass through certain doors, a blind to certain eyes. It was a disguise woven from the very fabric of our captivity. But it would only fit one of us.

“I will go,” I said. “I am closest to his size. And if I am caught…” I did not finish. If I was caught, Lucia and Matteo might still have a chance, hidden here with their captive.

Lucia did not argue. She helped me strip the man of his outer tunic, his breeches, his cap. The clothes were foul from the bucket, and too broad in the shoulder, but they carried the authority of grime and routine. I pulled them on. The smell was a nauseating cloak.

“Where do I go?” I asked, the question hanging in the cell. Freedom was not a gate out of the Arsenal. It was a specific, impossible destination. “We cannot just flee. Barbaro is still there. The record is still being written.”

Lucia’s eyes met mine. They were black pools in the dim light, but they held a fierce, focused light. “The records room,” she said. “You must go back.”

The absurdity of it was like a physical blow. Back into the dragon’s throat.

“His deposition,” she insisted. “He is writing it now. It is the source of his truth. It is the tool he will use to erase us. You must get to it. Not to steal it. To amend it.”

“Amend it? With what?”

“With a different truth,” Matteo grunted, gripping his injured arm. “With his truth.”

And I understood. Barbaro’s power lay in the official narrative. To fight him, we could not use force. We had to use his own weapon. We had to insert a contradiction into his perfect record. We had to make the story he was telling untenable, not by denying it, but by complicating it with a fact he could not explain away. We had to find the crack in his own archive.

“The original,” I breathed. “The vellum sheet. The one with the Doge’s seal, ordering the cessation. He kept it. It is the proof of his disobedience, of the corruption he serves. If that document were to appear in the hands of the Council of Ten, attached to his deposition…”

“His neat story unravels,” Lucia finished. “He is no longer a custodian catching spies. He is a conspirator caught destroying state orders. The tradition becomes treason.”

It was a plan spun from spider-silk. It required me to walk, in a stinking jailer’s disguise, back into the heart of the administrative wing, to find a document Barbaro would have hidden with fanatical care, and to somehow place it where it would be discovered. It was impossible.

It was also the only innovation left to us.

I took the jailer’s keys, selecting the one that matched our cell, leaving the rest. I pulled the cap low over my brow. I looked at Matteo, at Lucia. No words were adequate. We were past them. We were a chord, stretched to breaking, waiting to see what note it would sound upon the snap.

I unlocked the door, opened it a crack. The corridor was empty, lit by a single guttering torch. The air was cooler, smelling of damp stone and old smoke. I slipped out.

The sound of the lock turning behind me was the sound of a world closing. I was alone in the bowels of the machine. I walked with the slumped, trudging gait I had observed, the keys jangling at my hip. I descended a short flight of steps, turned a corner. A guard stood at a junction, leaning on his spear. He glanced at me, at my soiled clothes, and nodded, a look of mild disgust on his face. The disguise worked. I was part of the furniture of filth and duty.

I climbed toward the daylight, following the memory of our forced march from the night before. The sounds of the Arsenal grew—the shriek of saws, the thunder of forges, the rhythmic thump of caulking mallets. It was the sound of the Republic’s muscle, flexing. I moved through it like a ghost, a piece of grit in the gear-train.

Reaching the administrative courtyard was like surfacing from a dive. The light was harsh, the air sharper. I kept my head down, my pace purposeful. The door to the records wing was ahead. A different guard stood there. My heart hammered against my ribs.

I did not break stride. I approached as if I belonged, grunting a vague, “Message for the Capi,” in the roughest Venetian I could muster.

The guard eyed me, his nose wrinkling. “You stink of the Cane.”

“Spilled the bucket,” I muttered, shrugging.

He shook his head in contempt and stepped aside. “Don’t track it on the floors.”

And I was inside. The silence here was different—a hushed, papery silence, the quiet of concentrated thought and hidden power. I knew the way. Past the first chamber of scribes, their pens scratching like insect legs. Down the corridor to the heavier door, the one that led to the older, deeper records.

It was here I stopped. This door would be locked. And I did not have the key. I could not pretend my way past this. I pressed my ear to the wood. No sound from within.

I had no tools. Only my hands, and the clothes I wore, and the desperate need inside me. I looked at the lock. It was similar to the one on our cell, but older, more ornate. A serratura a doppia mordacchia indeed. I thought of Lucia, of her fingers tracing mortar. I thought not of picking it, but of its place in the system. This door was opened by certain people, at certain times. It would be opened soon, by a clerk, by Barbaro himself.

I could not wait. I stepped back, looked around. A narrow, arched passage led off to the left, likely for servants, for the removal of ash from braziers. I took it. It wound behind the records rooms, a dark, close space smelling of dust and old wax. I counted doors, trying to align them with my memory of the chamber where we had been captured.

Then I heard it. The sound of a voice. Barbaro’s voice. It was muffled, but clear, coming from a gap where a warped door did not quite meet its frame. I crept closer, my breath held.

“…the three individuals, apprehended in the act of tampering with the classified inventories pertaining to the Galea di Fortuna,” he was dictating. The smooth, assured tone of a man writing history. “Their possession of a forged Arsenal insignia confirms their intent to deceive and infiltrate. The state’s security…”

He was in a side-chamber, an annex to the main records room. He was alone, speaking to a scribe. I peered through the crack.

Barbaro stood by a high desk, his back to me. A young scribe sat, pen flying across paper. On a smaller table beside them lay a familiar, wax-stained leather folio. Our folio. And beside it, a single, heavy sheet of vellum. Even from this distance, I could see the dark, dangling impression of the Doge’s seal.

My proof. His death warrant.

But it was three paces from him. In a locked room. With him in it.

I watched, a statue of grime and stolen cloth. He finished a paragraph, paused. “Let me review the section on the recovered items,” he said to the scribe. He turned and walked to the main records room, leaving the door between ajar. The scribe remained, head bent, pen poised.

This was the moment. A sliver of time, thinner than a luthier’s blade. I had no plan, only a direction: forward.

I pushed the servant’s door open. It groaned softly. The scribe looked up, his eyes widening at the sight of a filthy jailer in the archive. I put a finger to my lips, a gesture of urgent, conspiratorial silence. I pointed frantically back the way I had come, then at the folio on the table.

“Il Capo,” I whispered, the words rough and hurried. “He needs it. The folio. For the Council. Now. In the main hall.”

The scribe’s confusion was profound. He looked from me to the folio, to the open door where Barbaro had gone. Protocol warred with the urgency in my stained face. The authority of the Casa del Cane, however lowly, was still authority.

“He said now,” I insisted, taking a step into the room, my hand outstretched.

The scribe, flustered, made a decision. He snatched up the folio—and with it, automatically, the vellum sheet that lay beneath, using it as a backing to carry the heavier papers. He thrust the bundle at me. “Be quick! And do not let him see the state of you.”

I clutched the precious bundle to my chest, turned, and vanished back into the servant’s passage. I did not run. I walked with the same trudging step, my heart a wild drum against the stolen documents. I had it. The original order. The seed of the corruption.

But I was not free. I was in a maze with the evidence in my hands, and the architect of that maze was now, undoubtedly, discovering the absence of his central exhibit. The silence behind me was about to shatter.

I had the truth. But truth, in the Arsenal, was a currency that had to be spent in the right chamber, before the right eyes. I had to get it to the Council of Ten. And between me and that impossibly lofty destination stood the entire, waking, hunting mechanism of Daniele Barbaro’s tradition.

I reached the administrative courtyard. The light seemed accusing now. I had to change direction. I could not go back to the Casa del Cane. That path was closed. I had to go up, and out, into the Venetian world beyond these walls. But the Arsenal had only two gates: the land gate and the water gate. Both would be watched, especially once the alarm was raised.

An idea, another desperate improvisation, struck me. I turned not toward the gates, but toward the noise, toward the squeri where the new galleys were built. Toward the smell of pine sap and the sound of adzes.

The Galea di Fortuna was there, in its dry dock, a skeleton awaiting its skin. It was the heart of the mystery, the silent vessel around which all this death and secrecy had coiled. And it was a public project, visited by officials, by patrons, by the curious. It was a place where a man in dirty clothes might not be too out of place.

I moved, the vellum sheet a burning brand against my skin. Behind me, from the records wing, a sharp cry went up. It was not a shout of alarm, not yet. It was a query, a note of pure, professional disbelief. It was the sound of a perfect record discovering a flaw.

The hunt was beginning. I had stolen the dragon’s tooth, and now I had to plant it in the dragon’s own bed, before the dragon consumed me. I was no longer just escaping. I was writing a marginal note in Barbaro’s text, a gloss of chaos on his page of order. And the only ink I had was my own life, running out with every step toward the looming, ribbed shadow of the unfinished ship.



---

Chapter 20

CHAPTER 20

The administrative cry faded behind me, swallowed by the greater, hungrier noise of the squeri. Here, the air was a broth of steam and resin, the wet-dust smell of fresh-sawn oak, the acid bite of hot pitch. The dry dock of the Galea di Fortuna was a vast, timber-lined pit, a wound in the Arsenal’s flesh. The ship within was not a vessel but a geometry of shadows, a cathedral of ribs rising from the keel like the bones of a leviathan dredged from the deep. Men swarmed over it, ant-like, their mallets and adzes a percussive counterpoint to the deeper, rhythmic groaning of ropes and pulleys.

I moved into the chaos as if stepping into a river. A labourer, his back a map of sweat and old scars, shoved a handcart of treenails past me, the iron-rimmed wheels chewing the damp earth. I did not look at him. To look was to invite inquiry. I kept my gaze on the ship, my pace that of a man with a purpose just vague enough to be unquestioned. My clothes—the stained tunic and breeches of a arsenalotto—were my only passport here. The vellum in my tunic was a cold, flat stone against my heart.

The plan was madness, a shape formed from panic and the memory of Matteo’s voice years ago, explaining the anatomy of a commission. The patrons come, he’d said, shaving a viol’s belly to a translucent thinness. They come to see their money made flesh, or wood. They bring their secretaries, their architects. They point and murmur. A man who looks like he belongs, who holds a board or a chalk-line, is invisible to them. He is part of the scenery.

I needed to become scenery. And then I needed to find a crack in that scenery, a way to insert the truth into the official view.

A foreman’s voice barked above the din, a whip-crack of Venetian dialect. “The stern-post! Is it seated? Or are you waiting for the Maggior Consiglio to come and kiss it into place?”

Laughter, rough and short. I used the sound as cover, ducking under a massive, curved frame that lay on trestles like a fossilised whalebone. The sap was fresh here, sticky and pungent. I reached out, my fingers brushing the raw wood, and came away with a palm smeared gold. I rubbed it on my tunic, on my cheek. The smell clung to me, a better disguise than any cloak.

From beneath the skeleton of the ship, I looked back the way I had come. Figures moved at the edge of the courtyard, their outlines sharp against the pale stone of the records office. They were not running. They were converging, a slow, deliberate clotting of officialdom. The query was becoming a search pattern.

My time was measured in heartbeats, in the draws of breath that tasted of pitch-pine and dread.

A group of men approached the dry dock from the landward side, their pace measured, their clothing a sombre palette of blacks and deep blues. Not workers. Officials. At their centre was a tall, slender man in a robe of fine wool, his hands clasped behind his back. He carried no papers, needed no escort of bravi. His authority was in the cut of his shoulder, the angle of his chin. Daniele Barbaro.

He stopped at the lip of the dry dock, looking down at his creation. The noise around him did not diminish, but it changed its quality. The shouts became more purposeful, the hammer-blows more evenly spaced. A performance for the master of the theatre.

I shrank back into the forest of timber, my blood a cold slurry in my veins. He was here. Of course he was here. The Fortuna was his text, his great argument written in oak and hemp. The theft from his records would be a personal violation, a blasphemy. He would feel it in his bones, as I felt the absence of a finished instrument in my hands.

He began to descend the wooden stairs into the pit, his retinue following like a wake. They moved toward the keel, where the master shipwright and his assistants waited, heads slightly bowed. A discussion of joinery, of timetables. The perfect, mundane alibi.

I could not reach the Council from here. The gates were watched, the walls were high, and Barbaro’s eyes, though they seemed fixed on the rabbet of the keel, would be everywhere. The truth in my tunic was a dead weight. It had to become a living thing. It had to speak.

My gaze swept the dry dock, past the cauldrons of bubbling pitch, the racks of adzes and augers, the coils of manila rope thick as a man’s thigh. It landed on a small, open-sided shed near the stocks, a place for the clerks. A trestle table was strewn with parchments: diagrams, lists of timber, inventories of nails and cordage. A young man in an ink-stained apron stood there, head bent, making a notation.

A marginal note. A gloss on the text.

I edged around the periphery, keeping the hull of the Fortuna between me and Barbaro’s group. The clerk did not look up. His world was columns and figures, the abstract blood of the project. I came up beside the shed, pretending to examine a stack of planks. I could see the pages on his table. One was a schematic of the gun-deck, the ports drawn in precise, ghostly circles.

The vellum in my tunic seemed to pulse. It was a schematic of another kind.

From the keel, Barbaro’s voice carried, clear and dry, devoid of the warmth he used in the Casa del Cane. “The reinforcement here is adequate. But the grain in this futtock is questionable. I want it replaced.”

“Sì, messere,” the master shipwright said, his voice tight. “It will delay the stepping of the mast.”

“A delay is preferable to a failure at sea. The tradition does not accommodate compromise.”

The tradition. The word hung in the resinous air, a verdict.

The clerk made a note on his sheet. He sanded it, blew the dust away.

An idea, desperate and complete, crystallised. I did not need to deliver the vellum. I needed to translate it. To make it part of the ship’s own documentation, where it would be found not by me, but by the next set of official eyes, by the Council’s auditors, by the suspicious mind of a rival Provveditore. I needed to plant the dragon’s tooth in the dragon’s ledger.

I waited until the clerk gathered a sheaf of papers and hurried toward the shipwright, called by a gesture from his master. The shed was unattended.

I moved. Three strides and I was at the table. My hands were steady, a liar’s hands. I scanned the documents. A delivery manifest for Lombardy oak. A payroll list. A schedule of inspections. And there, half-under a weight, a large sheet of fine paper: the current overall plan of the Fortuna, updated to show today’s progress. It was a copy, a working copy, smudged with charcoal and thumbprints.

From my tunic, I drew the vellum. It crackled, a sound like distant fire. I unfolded it just enough to see the crucial diagram—the modified hull lines, the notations on displacement and stability, the damning calculations in Barbaro’s own hand that proved the Fortuna was not merely innovative, but dangerously, knowingly unstable. A ship designed to founder under a specific load of treasure.

I had no time for nuance. I took a charcoal stub from the table. On the clean margin of the shipyard plan, I began to copy, not the calculations, but the visual truth. The subtle, deadly curve of the hull that deviated from the official lines. I sketched it quickly, the charcoal whispering on the paper. I added no words. Only the shape, the wrong shape, and an arrow pointing to the keel where Barbaro now stood.

It was a scream in a silent language. An engineer would see it. A naval architect would see it. The Council of Ten, who trusted numbers and diagrams more than the ravings of a luthier, would see it.

Footsteps approached. The clerk returning.

I folded the vellum back into my tunic. I let the charcoal drop, a casual accident, and bent to pick it up as the clerk re-entered the shed.

“You,” he said, suspicion flattening his voice. “What is your business here?”

I straightened, the charcoal in my hand. I gestured vaguely toward the stack of planks. “Checking the stock for the master caulker. He wants the heartwood, not the sapwood. Says the Provveditore will have his head if a seam opens.”

The clerk frowned, his eyes darting to his table. He saw his papers, seemingly undisturbed. He saw the shipyard plan, now bearing its new, hidden annotation. He saw nothing out of place.

“Well, be quick. And do not touch the documents. The Provveditore is here.”

“I see him,” I said, injecting a note of awed fear into my voice. I nodded toward Barbaro, then shuffled back toward the timbers, my shoulders hunched.

The clerk grunted, dismissing me, and turned back to his work. He did not look at the plan. To him, it was just another sheet, already marred by the day’s work.

I melted back into the flow of bodies, my heart hammering against the vellum. The first step was done. The truth was no longer solely mine. It was embedded, a seed of chaos in the official record. But it was a fragile seed. It needed sunlight to grow. It needed to be found by the right hands before Barbaro’s men found me.

A new sound cut through the industrial din—not a tool, not a shout. A bell. A single, clear, deliberate toll from the direction of the land gate. Then another. It was the Marcilliana, the bell that signalled a change of watch, but the rhythm was wrong. It was too measured, too insistent.

A signal.

The activity in the dry dock did not cease, but it stuttered. Men looked up from their work, not at the gate, but at each other. The foreman’s voice rose again, sharper now. “Eyes on your work! That bell is not your mother calling you to supper!”

But it was a call to supper for the hunters. I saw two of the figures from the records office break away from the courtyard edge and start moving methodically down the lines of the squeri, their eyes scanning the faces of the workers. They were not asking questions. They were looking. For a face that did not fit, for a man with the stink of the archive still on him.

I was trapped in a bowl of earth, with the ship’s bones rising around me and the walls of the dock sheer. The only ways out were the stairs Barbaro had descended and a series of rough ladders used by the workers. All would be watched now.

My gaze climbed the monstrous ribcage of the Fortuna. The men on the upper frames were silhouettes against the grey-white sky, secured by ropes. They moved with a slow, swaying grace, high above the mud and the scrutiny.

Up. The only direction left.

I moved to the nearest ladder, a rough thing lashed to a framing timber. I climbed, not quickly, not slowly, but with the deliberate pace of a man sent on an errand. The wood was slick with sap and condensation. Below, the world flattened and receded. The sounds separated—the deep thud of mallets from the high ping of smaller hammers, the hiss of steam from the guttural shouts.

I reached a broad staging platform lashed to the ship’s side, level with the fifth or sixth rib. From here, I could see across the Arsenal. The land gate was a distant arch, a maw. Figures clustered there. The water gate, a darker slit leading to the lagoon, was similarly attended. The net was drawing tight.

And then I saw him. Not a hunter, but the quarry I had forgotten. Carlo.

He was on the opposite side of the dry dock, half-hidden behind a mountain of barrel staves. He was not looking at the gates or the searchers. He was looking at me. His face was a mask of grime and exhaustion, but his eyes were clear, fixed on my perch with a terrifying intensity. He had followed me. Or he had been driven here, as I had, by the same instinct for the heart of the maze.
